    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="98f6ba6c42964da28646f283f7265a08">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd9dca254-06b4-4aea-8077-da75d915880d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Gong/Xiangrui">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/You/Tingting" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a5" data-db-target-for="a5" aria-controls="a5" aria-haspopup="true" id="a5_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Prof. Tingting You</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a5" aria-labelledby="a5_Ctrl" role="region" id="a5">
                     <p class="author-name">Prof. Tingting You</p>
                     <p>School of Chemistry, Beihang University, Beijing, 100191 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/You/Tingting">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Jiang/Ying" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Prof. Ying Jiang<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d23252265" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>In sodium-ion batteries, the layered transition metal oxides used as cathode often experience interlayer sliding of interlayer spacing and lattice variations during charge/discharge, leading to structural damage and capacity degradation. To address this challenge, a La<sup>3+</sup> doping strategy guided by Bayesian optimization has been employed to prepare the high-performance O3-NaNi<sub>0.39</sub>Mn<sub>0.50</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>La<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMCL) cathode material. Density functional theory calculations reveal that the O&nbsp;2p orbital overlaps with the t<sub>2g</sub> orbital of transition metals in NMCL, facilitating the formation of Na−O−La bonds and promoting the oxygen redox reaction kinetics. During the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process, NMCL exhibits significant negative lattice expansion, characterized by an increase in the c lattice parameter and exceptionally low volume expansion of 1.8 % and 3.1 %, respectively. Consequently, it delivers an excellent specific capacity of 243.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> over a wide voltage range of 2.0 V to 4.5 V, which can be attributed to La<sup>3+</sup> doping that promotes oxidation of O<sup>2−</sup> to peroxide O<sub>2</sub><sup>n−</sup> (<i>n</i>&lt;2) during charge.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ange202424572-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ange202424572-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapid growth of electric vehicles, photovoltaics, and wind power has led to significant demands for cost effective electrochemical energy storage in the past decades.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0001_R_d23252257e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> In this context, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have garnered widespread attention due to their low cost and high volumetric energy density, however, their energy density and lifespan remain strongly constrained by the performance of the cathode material.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0005_R_d23252257e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Commonly studied cathode materials include layered transition metal (TM) oxide sodium salts (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>), Prussian blue analogs, and organic compounds.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0008_R_d23252257e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Within the category of Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, the materials can be classified into two structural types based on the configuration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions: octahedral (O) sites and trigonal prismatic (P) sites.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0011_R_d23252257e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> Among these, NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (0&lt;x, y&lt;1, x+y=1) stands out as a promising candidate due to its superior energy density.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0014_R_d23252257e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater.">5</a></sup></span> However, its cycling stability is restricted by the high charge cut-off voltage, which induces detrimental phase transitions and severe c-axis shrinkage.</p>
         
         <p>As reported, the doping strategy effectively controls lattice expansion during cycling by regulating the phase structures,<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0015_R_d23252257e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span> and has been extensively explored to enhance the performance of NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials. For instance, the reduced proportion of the emerging O′3 phase enables Cu<sup>2+</sup>/Ti<sup>4+</sup> codoped NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub> to achieve improved cycling stability during charge.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0015_R_d23252257e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span> At high voltage, Mn<sup>3+</sup> tends to migrate from TM to Na layers owing to the strong Jahn–Teller distortion effect of octahedral Mn<sup>3+</sup>-O<sub>6</sub>, leaving cation vacancies. These vacancies enable the formation of “vacancy-O-vacancy” configurations, which trigger oxygen redox reactions. For O3-NaTi<sub>0.25</sub>Fe<sub>0.25</sub>Co<sub>0.25</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, it undergoes an O3-O′3-P3-O3′′ phase transition pathway during charge from 2.0 to 4.2 V, with the OP2 phase observed at 4.2 V.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0016_R_d23252257e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span> As expected, the well-designed structure not only effectively suppresses the bulk and surface structural fractures caused by repeated lattice volume variations, but also enhances the kinetics of desodiation. However, to the best of our knowledge, current modification techniques still fall short of fully addressing the challenges of irreversible phase transitions and structural degradation in O3-type cathode materials.</p>
         
         <p>Given the strong component sensitivity of NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode materials towards structural stability, even small changes in doping could potentially lead to significant synergistic effects among TM components, thereby greatly enhancing charge/discharge performance. As a dopant in lithium-ion battery cathode materials, rare earth La<sup>3+</sup> can enrich the migration paths of alkali metal ions to improve the ionic conductivity,<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0017_R_d23252257e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> while low-activity Cu<sup>2+</sup> helps reduce side reactions between the electrolyte and freely dissolved Na<sup>+</sup>, contributing to the long-term cycling stability.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0020_R_d23252257e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Energy">9</a></sup></span> Hence, it is reasonably believed that La<sup>3+</sup> doping could make a great positive contribution to the performance of NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. However, the high compositional sensitivity also implies that optimizing the formula of NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub> through manual search would be challenging. Moreover, introducing even one more component would exponentially increase the workload.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, the Bayesian optimization (BO) algorithm has been successfully employed to expedite the optimization of the optimal La<sup>3+</sup> doping amounts in NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, with the goal of regulating phase evolution to achieve negative lattice expansion and excellent discharge performance. Finally, the optimal sample, O3-NaNi<sub>0.39</sub>Mn<sub>0.50</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>La<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (dubbed as NMCL), was identified after just two iterations comprising a total of 19 experiments, which delivers a remarkably high specific capacity of 243.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Our study used synchrotron X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and out-of-situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) to reveal the presence of oxygen redox during electrochemical processes. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) analysis shows that NMCL exhibits negative lattice expansion under high pressure, with a low volume expansion rate of 3.1 %. Furthermore, density functional theory (DFT) calculations support the charge compensation mechanism identified by XPS and XAS analyses. This novel approach undoubtedly opens new avenues for the design of advanced cathode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ange202424572-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ange202424572-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>BO was conducted to guide the optimization of battery performance by tuning the dosage of candidate raw materials. The results revealed a general trend where the discharge voltage is inversely proportional to the discharge capacity for a given elemental composition.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0021_R_d23252257e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span> Based on this insight, we selected discharge voltage as the BO target, aiming to minimize it to enhance discharge performance. Herein, n<sub>a</sub>, n<sub>b</sub>, n<sub>c</sub>, and n<sub>d</sub>=1−n<sub>a</sub>−n<sub>b</sub>−n<sub>c</sub> denote the molar weight of Ni(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>, Mn(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>, Cu(CH<sub>3</sub>COO)<sub>2</sub>, and La(CH<sub>3</sub>COOH)<sub>2</sub>, respectively. Particularly, we focused on the condition where the total amount of n<sub>a</sub> and n<sub>b</sub> exceeds 0.5 (n<sub>a</sub>+n<sub>b</sub>&gt;0.5) to ensure no crystal phase change. Under the constraints, n<sub>a</sub>+n<sub>b</sub>+n<sub>c</sub>+n<sub>d</sub>=1 and n<sub>a</sub>+n<sub>b</sub>&gt;0.5, our task was to identify the optimal combination of n<sub>a</sub>, n<sub>b</sub>, and n<sub>c</sub> that minimizes the discharge voltage. The optimal sample was synthesized via coprecipitation to form precursors Ni<sub>a</sub>Mn<sub>b</sub>Cu<sub>c</sub>La<sub>d</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, followed by calcination to crystallize the cathode material, NaNi<sub>a</sub>Mn<sub>b</sub>Cu<sub>c</sub>La<sub>d</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0001">1a</a>).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ange202424572-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9640dd6a-ac33-44bc-b557-461ce49b9e4d/ange202424572-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9640dd6a-ac33-44bc-b557-461ce49b9e4d/ange202424572-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8b90686a-6d9a-4f4c-b0fa-3a706723c2b8/ange202424572-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9640dd6a-ac33-44bc-b557-461ce49b9e4d/ange202424572-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202424572-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202424572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Schematic synthesis of La<sup>3+</sup> doped NaNi<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Discharge capacity vs. target component and discharge voltage for (b) n<sub>Ni</sub>, (c) nM<sub>n</sub>, (d) n<sub>Cu</sub>, and (e) n<sub>La</sub>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>We began by experimentally synthesizing six samples through random combinations of n<sub>a</sub>, n<sub>b</sub>, and n<sub>c</sub> to cover the composition space as uniformly as possible (Table S1). Based on the initial experimental data, the BO model recommended new elemental compositions for subsequent rounds of experimentation. Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0001">1b</a> to <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0001">1e</a> display the discharge capacity and voltage measurements for the 19 synthesized samples. The solid spheres represent experimental data, including the six initial data and 13 iteratively recommended samples. The color gradient, ranging from blue to red, indicates an increase in discharge capacity. The projected points of experimental data on the corresponding two-dimensional plane are also provided, showing the 13 samples recommended across three rounds of optimization (Figure S1). These projections illustrate that, for achieving higher discharge capacity, the optimal composition region is approximately n<sub>a</sub>=0.3–0.4 (Ni), n<sub>b</sub>=0.5–0.6 (Mn), and n<sub>c</sub>+n<sub>d</sub>=0–0.2 (Cu+La). Eventually, the convergent optimal composition was determined to be n<sub>a</sub>=0.3891, n<sub>b</sub>=0.5033, n<sub>c</sub>=0.0588, and n<sub>d</sub>=0.0488, in agreement with the inductively coupled plasma (ICP) analysis of NMCL (Table S2). Notably, NMCL features a high discharge capacity of 243.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a low discharge voltage of 2.64 V. The XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement results for NMCL and the control sample of NaNi<sub>0.39</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NMC) (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0002">2a</a> and S2) show that NMCL adopts a mixed phase of R-3c and R-3 m space groups, and La element occupies the Mn 3b position (Table S3 and S4).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ange202424572-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/02f2cbb3-31f4-45b9-b400-7ab11fc91f70/ange202424572-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/02f2cbb3-31f4-45b9-b400-7ab11fc91f70/ange202424572-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8b4f2069-85ba-4ac7-88ff-4450b0e426ff/ange202424572-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/02f2cbb3-31f4-45b9-b400-7ab11fc91f70/ange202424572-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202424572-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202424572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Rietveld refinement and XRD patterns, (b) Crystal structures, (c<sub>1</sub>, c<sub>2</sub>) HRTEM images and the SAED of NMCL, and (c<sub>3</sub>) its corresponding strain distrib-ution, (d<sub>1</sub>, d<sub>2</sub>) HRTEM images and the SAED of NMC, and (d<sub>3</sub>) its corresponding strain distribution.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0002">2b</a> presents the related crystal structures of the mixed phase, which facilitates the formation of abundant oxygen vacancies while inhibiting oxygen loss during cycling. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (Figure S3) show that NMCL and NMC exhibit a similar morphology with particle sizes around 1 μm. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0002">2c</a><sub>1</sub>) and its inverse fast Fourier transform (IFFT) mode (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0002">2c</a><sub>2</sub>) for NMCL show an interlayer spacing approximately 6.75 Å for the O3 phase and 2.17 Å for NiMnLaO<sub>3</sub> (NML), as well as an obvious crystal interface between the two phases. The bright spots in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (inset of Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0002">2c</a><sub>2</sub>) confirm the existence of O3<sub>(100)</sub>, O3<sub>(002)</sub>, O3<sub>(102)</sub> facets, along with hexagonal NML<sub>(202)</sub> crystal planes. In contrast, NMC displays lattice fringes corresponding only to the O3 phase. More importantly (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0002">2d</a><sub>1</sub>, d<sub>2</sub>), the stress distribution in NMCL differs significantly from that in NMC (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0002">2c</a><sub>3</sub>, d<sub>3</sub>). Geometric phase analysis (GPA) reveals that NMC exhibits greater stress fluctuations, with stress accumulation caused by phase transition leading to crack formation and accelerated dissolution of TM ions during the charge/discharge cycles. In contrast, NMCL displays a relatively uniform stress distribution, indicating that the introduction of La<sup>3+</sup> effectively mitigates internal stress changes in the material. This alleviation enhances electron transport and improves electrochemical kinetics. Additionally, elemental mapping confirms the uniform distribution of Na, La, Mn, Ni, Cu, and O (Figure S4).</p>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">b</a> present the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles in the voltage range of 2.0−4.5 V that NMCL delivers a much higher specific capacity of 243.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> than 182.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of NMC at 0.1 C. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) profiles of the two samples exhibit similar shapes (Figure S5). The higher intensity of the oxygen-related peaks in NMCL indicates that the oxygen redox process should contribute significantly to its higher capacity compared to NMC.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0022_R_d23252257e895" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span> With increasing rates, the discharge specific capacities of NMCL reach 243.3, 220.7, 179.8, 147.3, 132.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.5, and 1 C, respectively, outperforming those of NMC, as shown in Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3c</a>. Furthermore, measurements using the Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) demonstrate faster ion transport kinetics compared to NMC (Figure S6).<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0023_R_d23252257e904" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) demonstrate a significant improvement in electrical conductivity after doping (Figure S7 and Table S5). This should be attributed to the widening of the interlayer spacing, in consistence with the GITT results. NMCL delivers a discharge capacity of 182.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 C and the highest average operating voltage of 2.985 V with a high energy density of 697.4 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, it exhibits a retention rate of 60.4 % after 100 cycles at 1 C, which is significantly better than 32.4 % of NMC, implying the superior structural stability and reduced lattice distortion in NMCL (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0026_R_d23252257e915" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Technol.">13</a></sup></span> After 100 cycles at 1 C, the corresponding impedance values (Figure S8 and Table S6) demonstrate that NMCL maintains a relatively low impedance, ensuring favorable ion transport, which is consistent with the findings in Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3e</a>. We examined the morphologies of NMC and NMCL electrodes after 100 cycles at 1 C. NMCL samples retain excellent structural integrity (Figure S9).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ange202424572-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7a535170-e68f-49b2-aab3-fa88257613c0/ange202424572-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7a535170-e68f-49b2-aab3-fa88257613c0/ange202424572-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1a4de22f-580e-4e69-b731-e45a11424b5d/ange202424572-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7a535170-e68f-49b2-aab3-fa88257613c0/ange202424572-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202424572-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202424572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of NMCL and NMC: (a) charge/discharge profiles, (b) cycling tests at 0.1 C, (c) rate capability, (d) cycling tests at 1 C, Evaluations on the full cell device based on O3-NMCL cathode and hard carbon anode (e) CV and galvanostatic charge/discharge curves, (f) galvanostatic charge–discharge curves, (g) cycling performance, and (h) rate capability of the full battery.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To explore the practical applicability of NMCL, we further assembled a sodium-ion full cell using the O3-NMCL cathode and hard carbon (HC) anode. By analyzing the CV and charge/discharge profiles of the two paired electrodes (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3e</a>), we set the full cell voltage window to 2.0−4.4 V and fixed the active mass ratio of the anode to cathode at 1 : 2.2. Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3f</a> and <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3g</a> display the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves and cycling life of the full cell. Its first discharge capacity is 116.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (based on the cathode), achieving an average working voltage of 3.03 V, corresponding to a specific energy density of 354.22 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, the full cell exhibits excellent rate performance, with a capacity of 104.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 C and 65 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0003">3h</a>). The performance of NMC||HC full cell is shown in Figure S10.</p>
         
         <p>In situ XRD has been used to monitor the structural evolution of NMCL and NMC during the 2.0−4.5 V charge/discharge cycles (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0004">4a</a>, <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0004">b</a>). During the initial charge process, NMCL undergoes an O3−P3 phase transition, as indicated by the evolution of the diffraction peaks of (003), (110), and (113). Upon charging to 4.0 V, the (003) peak shifts to a higher diffraction angle and widens, which is likely caused by increased O−O repulsive forces and the formation of OP2 accumulation. This observation is consistent with the increase in the oxidation state of TMs under high voltage and the reduction in the residual Na<sup>+</sup> content. The moderate P3−OP2 phase transition at high voltage is more favorable for structural stability compared to the P3−O1 or O3′ phase transitions in Figure S11.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0027_R_d23252257e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Appl. Therm. Eng.">14</a></sup></span> The original O3 phase is fully restored when discharged to 2.0 V, experiencing a reversible phase transition. The asymmetric pathway can be attributed to the different Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation kinetics in the Na layers of the OP2 phase. Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0004">4c</a> illustrates the schematic process of crystal structure change. During the early phase transition stage of the OP2 phase, the prismatic layer distance is wider than that of the octahedral layer because Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the face-shared prismatic sites is faster than through the edge-shared octahedral sites.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0028_R_d23252257e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span> As the alkalization progresses, the decrease in oxygen repulsion results in the shrinkage of the prismatic interlayer, incorporating more Na<sup>+</sup>, while the octahedral interlayer expands by 2.2 %. The negative lattice expansion causes a low volume change of 3.1 % during the O/P intergrowth, which effectively alleviates lattice distortion, improving the cycling performance (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0004">4d</a>). Correspondingly, the volume change of NMC is 10.49 %.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ange202424572-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/611a12ac-0c97-430d-9d29-2fa9bda174d9/ange202424572-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/611a12ac-0c97-430d-9d29-2fa9bda174d9/ange202424572-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/22ab8fe7-2f8c-4d67-8903-e0fff4102581/ange202424572-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/611a12ac-0c97-430d-9d29-2fa9bda174d9/ange202424572-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202424572-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202424572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a, b) In situ XRD patterns of NMCL and NMC, (c) schematic diagram of the crystal structure change progress, (d) the c-axis lattice parameters and the volume evolution during charge/discharge of NMCL.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The charge compensation across different states in the 2.0–4.5 V range has been investigated by <i>ex situ</i> XPS. During the charging process, the peaks of Mn&nbsp;2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn&nbsp;2p<sub>1/2</sub> shift towards higher binding energies (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0005">5a</a>), indicating the formation of Mn<sup>4+</sup>. When the voltage exceeds 4.0 V, the rapid increase in Mn<sup>4+</sup> is attributed to the distortion and rearrangement of the crystal structure. This transformation not only suppresses the phase transition but also mitigates the Jahn–Teller effect caused by Mn<sup>3+</sup>, thereby enhancing the energy density. As shown in Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0005">5b</a>, the Ni&nbsp;2p spectrum of NMCL indicating the valence state of Ni is initially Ni<sup>2+</sup>. When charged to 3.45 V, both peaks shift towards higher binding energies, and a peak corresponding to Ni<sup>4+</sup> appears at 858.5 eV. At the same time, the content of Ni<sup>3+</sup> decreases significantly. However, the La<sup>3+</sup> element remains inert and does not participate in the charge/discharge processes (Figure S12).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ange202424572-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6c223f1c-c1cc-40d2-98d1-c396467e61a7/ange202424572-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6c223f1c-c1cc-40d2-98d1-c396467e61a7/ange202424572-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/320153f6-3f77-4eb1-9e3a-f2bbc0fb5e53/ange202424572-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6c223f1c-c1cc-40d2-98d1-c396467e61a7/ange202424572-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202424572-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202424572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Mn&nbsp;2p (the binding energies of Mn&nbsp;2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mn&nbsp;2p<sub>1/2</sub> are 641.6 and 653.1 eV) (b) Ni&nbsp;2p (the binding energies of Ni&nbsp;2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni&nbsp;2p<sub>1/2</sub> are 854.4 and 871.6 eV), (c) O&nbsp;1 s XPS spectra of NMCL at difference charged and discharge states during the first cycle. (d) Schematic representation of the LaO<sub>6</sub> distoriton in the charged NMCL structure viewed along the (001) direction, and the blue, red and yellow indicate the La, oxygen and Na atoms, respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The Cu&nbsp;2p spectrum (Figure S13) shows that Cu<sup>3+</sup> is formed during the charge/discharge processes. The O&nbsp;1s spectrum reveals two distinct oxygen species: surface oxygen and lattice oxygen, (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0005">5c</a>). During charge, a new peak appears at around 530.7 eV, attributed to the formation of (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup> species. Upon further discharge, the peak of (O<sub>2</sub>)<sup>n−</sup> disappears, indicating the high reversibility of lattice oxygen redox in NMCL. The doping of La<sup>3+</sup> into the TM layers promotes the oxidation of lattice oxygen, resulting in the formation of Na−O−La bonds. This is reflected in the reduced distance between O−O layers and the creation of trapped lattice oxygen. In other words, the OP2 phase serves as the boundary phase between O3 and P3 phase transitions. The OP2 phase not only facilitates reversible migration but also enables the reversible removal of lattice oxygen, distinguishing between reversible lattice oxygen redox and irreversible lattice oxygen loss.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0029_R_d23252257e1103" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Carbon Energy">16</a></sup></span> This remarkable effect is attributed to the Na−O−La modulation of adjacent stacking patterns (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0005">5d</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The influence of La<sup>3+</sup> doping on the electronic structure and phase evolution behavior was investigated using DFT calculations. Upon Na<sup>+</sup> extraction, the projected density of states (PDOS) of Ni and Mn in NMCL progressively shifts towards higher energy levels, indicating that both Ni and Mn elements contribute to charge compensation during cycling (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6a</a> and <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6b</a>). Meanwhile, a strong coupling exists among the O&nbsp;2p, Mn&nbsp;3d, and Ni&nbsp;3d orbitals on both sides of the Fermi level, promoting ion migration and contributing to a relatively high discharge capacity. When Na is 0.58 and 0.41, the energy levels above the Fermi level increase significantly, indicating the generation of numerous oxygen vacancies. This supports the involvement of lattice oxygen in charge compensation.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0030_R_d23252257e1122" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span> This result is consistent with the decrease in the Bader charge as Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted, further confirming this conclusion (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6c</a>). Specifically, the average Bader charge valence state of O decreases from 1.180 to 1.154 with the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup>, which directly indicates the formation of many O&nbsp;2p electron holes.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ange202424572-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cecea489-3b18-4915-af5b-131fc7d436fb/ange202424572-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cecea489-3b18-4915-af5b-131fc7d436fb/ange202424572-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2dbba093-46e4-4ecd-935f-695de752b22d/ange202424572-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cecea489-3b18-4915-af5b-131fc7d436fb/ange202424572-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ange202424572-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fange.202424572" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a, b) Comparison of PDOS of Mn&nbsp;3d, Ni&nbsp;3d and O&nbsp;2p in NMCL (<i>x</i>=1, 0.58, 0.41), (c) the calculated average Bader charge of the O, (d,e) O K-edges XAS spectra of NMCL at different charged and discharged states collected through TEY mode, (f) schematic of the orbital combinations between TM and O, (g) electron localization function of valence electrons for Na<sub>x</sub>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The oxygen redox behavior was investigated through the <i>ex situ</i> O K-edge soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) in the total electron yield (TEY) mode. As shown in Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6d</a> and <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6e</a>, the pre-edge peak at 529 eV is attributed to electron transitions from O&nbsp;1 s to the hybrid bands of O&nbsp;2p-TM&nbsp;3d(t<sub>2g</sub>) and O&nbsp;2p-TM&nbsp;3d(e<sub>g</sub>) bands.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0031_R_d23252257e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18">18</a></sup></span> In Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6d</a>, the pre-edge peak at 533.5 eV is attributed to the presence of carbonaceous components or the formation of sodium oxide in the electrode.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0034_R_d23252257e1182" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Commun.">19</a></sup></span> The shoulder peak at 531.9 eV indicates that the generation of additional unoccupied states related to holes in the O&nbsp;2p orbitals during the (de)intercalation process.<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0035_R_d23252257e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span> The disappearance of the shoulder peak upon discharging to 2.0 V indicates that the oxygen redox reaction in NMCL is reversible. In contrast, the absence of the shoulder peak (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6e</a>) suggests that the oxygen redox reaction does not occur in NMC. As described in Figure S14, the strong reduction peak near 4.3 V in the dQ/dV curve further demonstrates the involvement of oxygen redox in NMCL. Additionally, the decrease in the Bader charges of Mn and Ni suggests that these elements participate in the charge transfer process, tending to donate electrons and acting as the primary redox centers (Figure S15).</p>
         
         <p>The projected density of states of the Cu&nbsp;3d and La&nbsp;3d orbitals contribute relatively little to the structural stability within the energy range of −4 to 4 eV, which plays a role in maintaining stability during the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process (Figure S16, 17).<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0036_R_d23252257e1197" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Energ. Fuel">21</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the La−O bond with a bond length of 2.27 Å is a weak covalent bond, leading to an increase in electron density around the O atoms. As a result, the Na−O−La configuration facilitates the reversible oxygen redox reaction, enhancing both electronic conductivity and crystal stability. The increase in electron density also causes the interlayer spacing to expand, which is the main reason for inducing the negative lattice expansion phase transition from P3 to OP2 (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6f</a>). Moreover, the relative changes in the interlayer distance further supported the conclusion drawn in Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0004">4b</a>. Both the experimental results and theoretical calculations confirm that oxygen participates in the charge compensation of NMCL.</p>
         
         <p>The mechanism of anion oxidation is further explained through the molecular orbital diagram (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6g</a>). The doping of La<sup>3+</sup> causes the O&nbsp;2p orbital to overlap with the TM t<sub>2g</sub> orbital. Due to the high electronegativity of Ni, the electrons in the unbonded O&nbsp;2p orbital transition to occupy the antibonding orbital at the top of the valence band, thereby enhancing the oxidation activity of lattice oxygen. To clarify the charge transfer of O, the electron localization function (ELF) maps of Na<sub>x</sub> were calculated (Figure <a href="#ange202424572-fig-0006">6h</a>, Figure S18). The results show that, in the cathode system, the Cu, Ni, and La atoms are reduced, while the O atoms are oxidized, with the electron cloud density around O decreasing. This is attributed to the overlap between the O&nbsp;2p orbital and the t<sub>2g</sub> orbital of TMs, which promotes the oxidation of lattice oxygen. Regarding phase transitions and oxidation, this change affects the atomic interactions and the reactivity of oxygen atoms. Compared with the representative nickel-manganese-based cathode materials, NMCL displays a higher specific discharge capacity and smaller lattice expansion (Figure S19 and Table S7).<span><sup><a href="#ange202424572-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0002_R_d23252257e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1a - Adv. Energy Mater.">1a</a>, <a href="#ange202424572-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ange202424572-bib-0037_R_d23252257e1224" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22">22</a></sup></span> Due to the delayed adverse phase transition, these reported cathodes could only deliver a capacity of no more than 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The reported NaNi<sub>0.68</sub>Mn<sub>0.22</sub>Co<sub>0.10</sub>O<sub>2</sub> achieves a relatively high capacity of 190 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 2.0−4.0 V, but it exhibits a significant lattice expansion of 4.6 %. In contrast, NMCL not only boasts a high capacity of 243.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> but also shows a negative lattice expansion of only 3.1 %, achieving both high capacity and good structural stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ange202424572-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ange202424572-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully employed the BO algorithm to develop La<sup>3+</sup> doped O3-NaNi<sub>0.39</sub>Mn<sub>0.50</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>La<sub>0.05</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a cathode material for SIBs. The elaborately designed NMCL demonstrates a high specific discharge capacity of 243.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 74 % after 100 cycles at 0.1 C across a wide voltage range of 2.0−4.5 V. The in situ XRD analysis has captured the phase transitions between O3, P3, and OP2, resulting in negative lattice expansion during charge/discharge. The lattice expansion of NMCL is only 3.1 %, which is beneficial for reducing lattice strain and enhancing structural stability. Furthermore, DFT calculations reveal that the overlap between O&nbsp;2p and TM orbitals stimulates lattice oxygen, forming Na-O−La configurations that promote oxygen redox reactions. The increased electron density enhances interlayer repulsion, inducing phase transitions and contributing to negative lattice expansion. This study offers new insights into advanced cathode materials that simultaneously increase specific capacity and minimize structural strain.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

Du</span>, <span class="author">H. M. Sun</span>, <span class="author">Y. Zhang</span>, <span class="author">Q. A. Liu</span>, <span class="author">T. T. Yang</span>, <span class="author">J. Zhao</span>, <span class="author">C. Delmas</span>, <span class="author">S. J. Harris</span>, <span class="author">H. L. Chen</span>, <span class="author">Q. Huang</span>, <span class="author">Y. F.
